{"parentUuid":"4d64db80-b7ba-472c-99db-d1838158b0d1","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"7fc8463a-44e2-406a-bf6d-b8eaf571a7cf","agentId":"a7e001d2642a2b970","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01TeMR6rJgUyLCPVvH4bFTwE","type":"tool_result","content":"# GTE Module Analysis\n\nThis VHDL implementation defines the **Geometry Transform Engine (GTE)** from the PlayStation 1 processor, a specialized coprocessor for 3D graphics calculations.\n\n## Core Functionality\n\nThe module implements matrix operations and geometric transformations including:\n- **RTPS/RTPT**: Perspective transformation with division\n- **MVMVA**: Matrix-vector multiplication with selectable matrices/vectors\n- **Color operations**: DPCS, INTPL, NCS, NCDS, CDP, CC for lighting and interpolation\n- **Arithmetic**: SQR, OP (outer product), NCLIP (clipping tests)\n- **Depth averaging**: AVSZ3, AVSZ4\n\nThe design processes \"mul1 mul2 add sub swap svSh useIR IRs IRsF satIR satIRF uRes trigger\" operations across multiple calculation states.\n\n## Optimization Techniques\n\n1. **Pipelined execution**: Multi-step state machine (calcStep 0-63) spreads calculations across cycles\n2. **Parallel MAC units**: Four independent multiply-accumulate blocks (MAC0-MAC3) compute simultaneously\n3. **Turbo mode**: Early exit option to reduce latency for time-critical operations\n4. **Resource sharing**: Single divider unit (\"div_trigger\") serves multiple operations\n5. **Batching**: Processes multiple vertices sequentially (batchCount 0-2) for DPCT, NCDT, NCCS variants\n\n## Approximations & Design Decisions\n\n- **Widescreen aspect ratio scaling**: Modifies IR1 via fixed-point arithmetic based on \"WIDESCREEN\" mode\n- **Flag saturation**: Selective clamping rather than full-precision results\n- **Shift operations**: Conditional right-shifting controlled by \"cmdShift\" parameter\n\n## FPGA Considerations\n\nThe implementation uses typical synthesis-friendly VHDL: 32/45-bit signed arithmetic, moderate register counts (~90+ registers for state storage), and synchronous logic clocked at 2x CPU frequency (clk2x). The extensive control logic and multiple calculation pathways consume significant LUT resources, though the modular MAC architecture enables reasonable tile efficiency on modern FPGAs like those used in MiSTer platforms."}]},"uuid":"269e42db-ed7d-4f82-a1a6-b905fcc176d1","timestamp":"2026-03-21T03:48:18.137Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"4d64db80-b7ba-472c-99db-d1838158b0d1","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Volumes/2TB_20260220/Projects/ROMs_Claude","sessionId":"5f0fda8f-0c14-4331-a220-93f2e2f75e51","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"indexed-squishing-parasol"}

The implementation handles:\n\n- **Drawing operations**: polygons, rectangles, lines, and filled areas\n- **Texture mapping** with filtering and palette support\n- **VRAM management**: CPU-to-VRAM, VRAM-to-VRAM, and VRAM-to-CPU transfers\n- **Video output**: interlaced/progressive modes, multiple resolutions, color depths\n- **State management**: savestate support for emulation pause/resume\n\n## Optimization Techniques\n\n**Pipeline Architecture**: The design separates concerns into specialized worker modules (line, rectangle, polygon processors) that operate concurrently rather than sequentially, improving throughput.\n\n**Dual-buffering Strategy**: Separate FIFOs handle input commands and output pixel writes, decoupling command processing from memory operations and preventing pipeline stalls.\n\n**Line RAM Caching**: \"ilineram\" dual-port RAM structures cache VRAM line data, reducing repeated memory accesses during texture operations.\n\n**Resource Sharing**: Multiple drawing units share divider circuits (\"gdividers\") rather than instantiating separate hardware for each processor.\n\n## FPGA-Specific Accommodations\n\n**Burst Transfers**: The module uses configurable burst counts (up to 128 entries) for efficient SDRAM access patterns, crucial for bandwidth-limited FPGA memory controllers.\n\n**Large FIFO Buffers**: \"SyncFifo\" with 256-entry depth handles broken homebrew software that exceeds original PSX FIFO limits without impacting official games.\n\n**Address Space Mirroring**: Support for dual SDRAM banks with configurable mirroring enables flexible memory configurations on different FPGA boards."}]},"uuid":"83c9f85d-2193-44fb-a728-560043c3ba40","timestamp":"2026-03-21T03:48:27.628Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"0d5006ec-77a0-401d-87ec-e90339e6a07d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Volumes/2TB_20260220/Projects/ROMs_Claude","sessionId":"5f0fda8f-0c14-4331-a220-93f2e2f75e51","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"indexed-squishing-parasol"}

It orchestrates:\n\n- **Three scrolling layers** with different tile sizes (8x8, 16x16, 32x32)\n- **Sprite rendering** (objects)\n- **Palette management** and color mixing\n- **Video timing** generation (sync signals, blanking intervals)\n\n## Resource Optimization Techniques\n\nThe design employs several efficiency strategies:\n\n1. **Conditional Compilation**: Extensive use of `ifdef` preprocessor directives allows selective inclusion of components. For CPS2 variants, object DMA is disabled with the comment \"People said that Progear was too slow,\" replacing complex logic with simplified assignments.\n\n2. **Time-Multiplexing**: The architecture separates concerns across dedicated modules (scroll, objects, color mixing, palette) that share a common video clock, avoiding redundant hardware.\n\n3. **Bus Arbitration**: A `busreq`/`busack` handshake manages VRAM access conflicts between DMA and rendering engines, serializing potentially competing requests.\n\n4. **Fake DMA Signals**: For CPU-only simulation modes (`NOMAIN`), the design generates synthetic DMA completion signals rather than implementing full DMA logic.\n\n5. **Parameterized Precision**: Object width (`OBJW`) and blanking delay (`BLNK_DLY`) vary by platform, optimizing for CPS1 versus CPS2 hardware differences."}]},"uuid":"c43518b6-32aa-4c52-9bea-cac0965830a5","timestamp":"2026-03-21T03:48:40.883Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"b05b1fd7-00f4-4000-a9b8-81992f3ee8c4","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Volumes/2TB_20260220/Projects/ROMs_Claude","sessionId":"5f0fda8f-0c14-4331-a220-93f2e2f75e51","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"indexed-squishing-parasol"}
{"parentUuid":"1f8b82cc-3fd7-44bd-8bd0-8e28e17859d6","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"7fc8463a-44e2-406a-bf6d-b8eaf571a7cf","agentId":"a7e001d2642a2b970","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01MZRxgX8q6WH1wj4R3Qc9ji","type":"tool_result","content":"# JTCPS1 Tilemap Module Architecture\n\nThis Verilog module implements a tilemap renderer for CPS1 arcade hardware with a sequential, time-multiplexed design to minimize resources.\n\n## Core Structure\n\nThe module processes tiles one at a time through a state machine (`st` counter) rather than processing multiple tiles simultaneously. It reads tile data from ROM in a controlled sequence: \"Scroll 1 is 512x512, 8x8 tiles; Scroll 2 is 1024x1024 16x16 tiles; Scroll 3 is 2048x2048 32x32 tiles.\"\n\n## Resource Sharing Strategy\n\n**Single ROM access path**: Rather than dedicating separate ROM readers per layer, the design uses one shared ROM interface with multiplexed requests. The state machine sequences through reading tile code, attributes, and pixel data with explicit wait states (state 5 is unused, forcing timing gaps).\n\n**Conditional mapper instantiation**: The module conditionally compiles three identical `jtcps1_gfx_mappers` instances only for CPS1 hardware; CPS2 variants use simplified assignments, demonstrating conditional resource usage based on target hardware.\n\n**Pixel shifting**: Pixel data extracts individual 4-bit colors through a `colour()` function applied during sequential writes, avoiding parallel pixel extraction logic.\n\n## Time-Multiplexing\n\nThe 50+ state sequence processes:\n- States 0-3: Tile lookup and attribute fetch\n- States 4-6: Initial ROM read\n- States 7-42: Pixel output (sequential 8-pixel writes per tile)\n- States 15, 24, 33: Layer-specific transitions\n\nDifferent tile sizes (8x8, 16x16, 32x32) reuse identical pixel-writing logic with conditional branching, consolidating hardware footprint rather than implementing parallel pipelines."}]},"uuid":"a25023cb-9ef4-4f83-a867-e9cb18b318c1","timestamp":"2026-03-21T03:48:44.593Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"1f8b82cc-3fd7-44bd-8bd0-8e28e17859d6","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Volumes/2TB_20260220/Projects/ROMs_Claude","sessionId":"5f0fda8f-0c14-4331-a220-93f2e2f75e51","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"indexed-squishing-parasol"}

It orchestrates the interconnection between processors, memory controllers, graphics subsystems, and audio components.\n\n## Major Subsystems Instantiated\n\nThe module instantiates several critical components:\n\n1. **Dual SH-7604 CPUs** (MSH/SSH) - Master and Slave processors with bus arbitration\n2. **SCU (System Control Unit)** - Bus controller managing address/data multiplexing\n3. **DCC (Dynamic CPU Controller)** - Memory chip select and wait state generation\n4. **VDP1/VDP2** - Graphics processors for sprite and background rendering\n5. **SMPC** - System Manager and Peripheral Controller for I/O and resets\n6. **SCSP** - Sound processor with integrated RAM and M68K audio CPU\n7. **CD subsystem** (non-STV builds) - CD drive controller with SH-1 and YGR019 chipsets\n8. **Cartridge Controller** - Handling different ROM cartridge formats, including STV variants\n\n## Optimization & Resource Considerations\n\nKey design features suggest resource-conscious implementation:\n\n- **Configurable RAMH timing**: The module accepts a `RAMH_SLOW` parameter to adjust memory performance\n- **Conditional compilation**: Uses preprocessor directives to support both Saturn and STV builds, reducing footprint for specific targets\n- **Bus arbitration logic**: Implements sophisticated wait-state handling to prevent conflicts\n- **Open bus emulation**: Maintains \"open bus\" state for unmapped address reads, improving accuracy without excess logic\n\nThe architecture demonstrates careful attention to fitting multiple complex subsystems within FPGA constraints."}]},"uuid":"79ab0028-563d-4530-8919-2091ba361ab8","timestamp":"2026-03-21T03:49:19.957Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"513ba015-281e-42db-9f64-1b8cf2b1ee1f","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Volumes/2TB_20260220/Projects/ROMs_Claude","sessionId":"5f0fda8f-0c14-4331-a220-93f2e2f75e51","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"indexed-squishing-parasol"}

The CPU operates with dual-phase clocking signals (`enPhi1` and `enPhi2`) derived from a 10MHz clock enable signal (`cen10` and `cen10b`).\n\n## Resource Sharing Techniques\n\n**Bus Arbitration**: The design implements sophisticated bus sharing through the `jtframe_68kdma` module, which manages contention between the CPU and other devices (like DMA controllers). This allows multiple components to safely access shared memory resources.\n\n**Address Space Multiplexing**: RAM and VRAM share contiguous SDRAM spaces, with logic that prevents overlapping: `\"assign addr = ram_cs ? {2'b0, A[15:1] } : A[17:1];\"` This optimization reduces external memory requirements by allowing the same physical storage to serve multiple logical functions.\n\n**Wait State Management**: The `jtframe_68kdtack_cen` module dynamically generates DTACK (Data Transfer Acknowledge) signals based on peripheral readiness, allowing slower ROM/RAM access without halting the CPU unnecessarily.\n\n## Key Optimizations\n\n- Conditional chip select signals reduce unnecessary memory transactions\n- Turbo mode support adjusts clock division (`4'd1 / 5'd4` vs. `4'd5 / 5'd24`)\n- Interrupt prioritization through IPL lines manages real-time events efficiently"}]},"uuid":"80e40560-9cf7-46a4-a576-be8a4bc28d90","timestamp":"2026-03-21T03:49:40.294Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"b280856e-f394-41b2-b192-f28e8bd34ba6","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Volumes/2TB_20260220/Projects/ROMs_Claude","sessionId":"5f0fda8f-0c14-4331-a220-93f2e2f75e51","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"indexed-squishing-parasol"}

Here's how it works:\n\n## Pipeline Structure\n\nThe operator processes data through multiple registered stages across 13+ cycles. As noted in the code: \"REGISTER/CYCLE 1\" through \"REGISTER/CYCLE 13\" show distinct processing stages including phase modulation, sine table lookup, and exponential conversion.\n\n## Data Flow Pattern\n\nThe module processes four operators sequentially: `m1 → c1 → m2 → S4` (as documented in the enters/exits comments). Between each operator, shift registers buffer intermediate results:\n\n- `prev1_buffer`, `prevprev1_buffer`, and `prev2_buffer` are \"jt51_sh\" shift register modules with 8 stages each\n- These buffers allow \"feedback signal\" data to flow through the pipeline\n\n## Resource Implications\n\nRather than sharing one operator circuit, this design uses **dedicated hardware**: separate ROM blocks for phase and exponential tables (`jt51_phrom`, `jt51_exprom`), arithmetic units, and shift registers all operate in parallel. The code was originally authored by Bryan McPhail and underwent a major rewrite in 2024.\n\n## Graphics Hardware Capabilities\n\nThe F3 hardware supports remarkably complex visual effects:\n\n**Layers & Sprites:**\n- \"4 scrolling layers (512x512 or 1024x512) of 4, 5, or 6 bpp tiles\"\n- One text layer with characters generated in VRAM (4bpp)\n- One pixel layer from pivot RAM (4bpp, 512x256 pixels)\n- Two sprite banks for double-buffering\n- Sprites supporting 4, 5, or 6 bpp color depths\n\n**Advanced Effects:**\n- Per-line sprite scaling and sprite bank switching\n- Line-by-line zoom/scaling on all playfields\n- Per-scanline effects for priority, mixing, row/column scrolling, clipping, and palette interpretation\n- Alpha blending with configurable contribution values\n- Mosaic/repeat effects\n\n## Complexity Assessment\n\nThis represents highly advanced arcade-era graphics hardware. The \"line ram\" memory structure enables extraordinary per-scanline customization—each of 256 screen lines can have independent zoom factors, scroll values, blending modes, and priority settings. {"parentUuid":"0270ec05-9c00-4cda-a125-057bdfdce3e1","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"c6f074d4-0ec7-4684-a899-043d3f613b8b","agentId":"a7e001d2642a2b970","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01UVujtTNsRdd2Fe9dTxYUqq","type":"tool_result","content":"# TC0630FDP Graphics Chip Analysis\n\nBased on the Taito F3 video driver documentation, here's what the TC0630FDP (F3 graphics processor) handles:\n\n## Memory Access\nThe chip manages several memory regions:\n- **Playfield RAM**: 4 scrolling tile layers (512x512 or 1024x512), supporting 4-6 bpp tiles\n- **Text/VRAM layer**: 512x512 character layer with 4bpp characters generated in VRAM\n- **Pivot RAM**: 512x256 pixel layer with 4bpp pixels\n- **Line RAM**: 256 entries controlling per-scanline effects across all layers\n- **Sprite RAM**: Two banks for double-buffering\n\n## Line RAM Structure\nThe documentation describes this as remarkably complex. Line RAM contains per-scanline control values at multiple address ranges:\n- Column scroll and alternate tilemap selection (0x4000)\n- Clipping planes (0x5000-0x5600)\n- Zoom factors for each playfield (0x8000-0x8600)\n- Palette adjustments (0x9000-0x9600)\n- Row scroll (0xa000-0xa600)\n- Layer priority/mixing information (0xb000-0xb600)\n\nEach scanline can have different zoom, scroll, clipping, and blending parameters.\n\n## Sprite System\n- **Count**: Up to 1024 sprites per frame (8-word entries)\n- **Tile sizes**: Variable, with scaling hardware\n- **Zoom**: Separate X/Y zoom per sprite, with scale factor: \"(256-zoom)/256\"\n- **Features**: Sprite trails (framebuffer persistence), flip modes, palette selection, special commands\n\n## Priority & Blending\nThe chip implements a sophisticated mixing system:\n- 4 playfield layers with per-line priority values\n- Sprite priority groups (4 levels: 0x00, 0x40, 0x80, 0xc0)\n- Per-line alpha blending with contribution values for source and destination\n- \"Saturating addition\" for pixel composition\n- Inverse clip modes and enable/disable per scanline\n\n## Complexity Assessment\nThis is **highly complex** for FPGA implementation. The per-scanline control architecture requires substantial state management, the 8-section line RAM demands parallel lookup capabilities, and the alpha blending circuit needs saturating arithmetic. The sprite scaling and multi-layer composition would require significant hardware resources."}]},"uuid":"854461dc-d515-4cfb-816a-de4ccb54d007","timestamp":"2026-03-21T03:53:44.115Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"0270ec05-9c00-4cda-a125-057bdfdce3e1","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Volumes/2TB_20260220/Projects/ROMs_Claude","sessionId":"5f0fda8f-0c14-4331-a220-93f2e2f75e51","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"indexed-squishing-parasol"}

Read `notes/session-log.md` — the slot layout is fully documented\n    86→2. Slot layout (confirmed): +0 state (word), +2 countdown (word), +4 PC (long), +8 A0 (long), +0C SR (word)\n    87→3. State values: 0=unoccupied, 1=counting, 4=expired/ready, 8=running, 0x0C=fresh-arm (first dispatch)\n    88→4. `timer_rearm()` in ffight.c should ONLY re-arm slots that were in state=4 BEFORE VBlank (use `pre_tX_state` snapshot)\n    89→5. `scheduler_step()` dispatches state=4 AND state=0x0C — both trigger entity_yield path\n    90→\n    91→**If entity output is wrong:**\n    92→1. Most divergence is MISSING (not WRONG/PHANTOM) — this is entity output not yet implemented, not bugs\n   101→4. WRONG or PHANTOM = actual code bug; investigate those first\n   102→\n   103→---\n   104→\n   105→## WHAT NOT TO DO (drift warning signs)\n   106→\n   107→- ❌ Writing large `if (frame_number >= N)` analytical branches → DELETE THESE\n   108→- ❌ Hard-coding specific byte values derived from diff output → use entity coroutines instead\n   109→- ❌ Running the same test 3+ times with tiny C changes between → stop, consult docs first\n   110→- ❌ Adding special cases for specific frame ranges → entity coroutines make this unnecessary\n   111→- ❌ Investigating MISSING diffs before WRONG/PHANTOM diffs → wrong priority\n   112→\n   113→---\n   114→\n   115→## CURRENT STATE (2026-03-17, Session 16)\n   116→\n   117→**Architecture:** Coroutine mode.     1→# MiSTer FPGA Gap Analysis — Top 5 Targets (Technical Deep Dive)\n     2→\n     3→## Target 1: Taito F3 (HIGHEST PRIORITY)\n     4→\n     5→### System Overview\n     6→- **Release**: 1992–1997\n     7→- **Processor(s)**: Motorola 68020 (16 MHz) + Z80 (4 MHz)\n     8→- **Audio**: Ensoniq ES5505 wavetable synthesizer\n     9→- **Custom Chips**:\n    10→  - **TC0630FDP** — Tilemap generator (massive Toshiba channel-less gate array)\n    11→  - **TC0400YSC** — CPU-to-sound interface\n    12→  - **TC0510NIO** — I/O controller\n    13→  - **TC0260DAR** — Palette RAM + DAC\n    14→- **Video**: 320×224 or 384×224, 16.7M color palette via TC0260DAR\n    15→- **Memory**: 2MB ROM, 64KB RAM, 32KB sprite RAM\n    16→- **Game Count**: 1,000+ titles (Rayforce, Liquid Kids, Bubble Memories, Elevator Action Returns, Puyo Puyo, Final Lap Racing, Kyukyoku Tiger, RaStanRessurection)\n    17→\n    18→### Architecture\n    19→```\n    20→68020 (16 MHz)\n    21→    ↓ (program ROM via banked 68000 address space)\n    22→    → ROM cartridge (2MB)\n    23→    → Tilemap/sprite generation (via TC0630FDP, TC0260DAR)\n    24→    → Screen output\n    25→\n    26→Z80 (4 MHz)\n    27→    ↓ (Ensoniq ES5505 sound ROM + RAM)\n    28→    → Yamaha TC0400YSC (comm)\n    29→    → Ensoniq ES5505 (8-voice wavetable)\n    30→    → Audio output\n    31→\n    32→I/O (TC0510NIO)\n    33→    ↓ (joystick, buttons)\n    34→    → Player 1, Player 2 input\n    35→```\n    36→\n    37→### Complexity Assessment\n    38→\n    39→**Hardware Complexity**: VERY HIGH (4+ custom ASICs)\n    40→- Tilemap generation (TC0630FDP) is the most challenging reverse-engineering task\n    41→- TC0400YSC (sound interface) is well-documented in MAME\n    42→- TC0510NIO (I/O) is straightforward\n    43→- TC0260DAR (palette/DAC) is standard\n    44→\n    45→**Software Complexity**: MEDIUM\n    46→- 68020 is mature in FPGA (though less common than 68000)\n    47→- Z80 is mature\n    48→- ES5505 is mature (used in Apple IIgs cores)\n    49→\n    50→**Custom Chip Reverse-Engineering Status**:\n    51→- ✅ TC0630FDP — Partially reverse-engineered; MAME has working implementation (source available)\n    52→- ✅ TC0400YSC — Fully documented; simple mailbox protocol\n    53→- ✅ TC0510NIO — Fully documented\n    54→- ✅ TC0260DAR — Fully documented; standard palette DAC\n    55→\n    56→### Implementation Roadmap\n    57→\n    58→**Phase 1 (Weeks 1–2): CPU + ROM + RAM**\n    59→- Integrate existing Motorola 68020 HDL (if available) or port from MiSTer 68000 core\n    60→- Integrate existing Z80 core from MiSTer\n    61→- Implement ROM cartridge banking (address space 0x000000–0x1FFFFF)\n    62→- Test basic CPU execution with diagnostic ROMs\n    63→\n    64→**Phase 2 (Weeks 3–4): I/O + Sound**\n    65→- Implement TC0510NIO (joystick/button input)\n    66→- Integrate existing Ensoniq ES5505 HDL (from Audio cores)\n    67→- Implement TC0400YSC mailbox protocol (Z80 ↔ 68020 communication)\n    68→- Test audio ROM loading and wavetable playback\n    69→\n    70→**Phase 3 (Weeks 5–8): Tilemap + Sprite Generation (Core Challenge)**\n    71→- Reverse-engineer TC0630FDP from MAME source code\n    72→- Implement TC0630FDP tilemap generation (horizontal + vertical scroll, line-scroll, column-scroll)\n    73→- Implement sprite rasterization (sprite buffer, priority mask)\n    74→- Implement TC0260DAR palette RAM + palette-per-scanline effects\n    75→- **Critical**: Cycle-accurate tilemap/sprite timing to match original hardware\n    76→\n    77→**Phase 4 (Week 9): Integration + Testing**\n    78→- Connect all modules (CPU, memory, I/O, audio, graphics)\n    79→- Run MAME cycle-accurate simulation side-by-side for validation\n    80→- Test 20–30 representative ROMs (Rayforce, Bubble Memories, Liquid Kids, Puyo Puyo)\n    81→\n    82→### MAME Reference Implementation\n    83→```c\n    84→// MAME tilemap generation (simplified)\n    85→void tc0630_draw_scanline(int scanline) {\n    86→    int scroll_x = read_ram(0xC00000);  // Horizontal scroll\n    87→    int scroll_y = read_ram(0xC00002);  // Vertical scroll\n    88→    int line_scroll_ptr = read_ram(0xC00004);  // Per-line scroll table\n    89→\n    90→    for (int x = 0; x < 320; x++) {\n    91→        int tile_x = (x + scroll_x) >> 3;\n    92→        int tile_y = (scanline + scroll_y) >> 3;\n    93→        int pixel_x = (x + scroll_x) & 7;\n    94→        int pixel_y = (scanline + scroll_y) & 7;\n    95→\n    96→        int tile_num = tilemap[tile_y][tile_x];\n    97→        int tile_data = chargen_rom[tile_num][pixel_y][pixel_x];\n    98→        int palette_idx = tile_data & 0xFF;\n    99→        int color = palette_ram[palette_idx];\n   100→        framebuffer[scanline][x] = color;\n   101→    }\n   102→}\n   103→```\n   104→\n   105→### Estimated Effort\n   106→- **Skilled FPGA Developer (with arcade background)**: 4–5 months, full-time\n   107→- **Expert (familiar with Taito cores)**: 2–3 months\n   108→- **Team (2–3 developers)**: 2–3 months (parallelizable: CPU/sound/I/O can be done in parallel)\n   109→\n   110→### Known Issues / Testing Challenges\n   111→1. **Cycle-accurate timing**: TC0630FDP output must align with beam position to 1-cycle accuracy\n   112→2. **Sprite priority**: Sprite layer + background layer blending requires careful bit ordering\n   113→3. **Palette per-scanline**: Some F3 games (Rayforce) use palette rotation effects per-scanline; complex timing\n   114→4. **68020 vs 68000 differences**: If using modified 68000 core, verify 32-bit extensions\n   115→\n   116→### Success Metrics\n   117→- ✅ All 1,000+ game ROMs load without crashes\n   118→- ✅ 90%+ of pixels match MAME output pixel-perfect (excluding timing-dependent effects)\n   119→- ✅ Audio plays without glitches\n   120→- ✅ Joystick input responsive (< 2 frames latency)\n   121→- ✅ Run at 60 FPS on DE10-Nano\n   122→\n   123→### Why This Target?\n   124→1. **Highest game count** of any untouched system (1,000+)\n   125→2. **Well-documented in MAME** — source code available as reference\n   126→3. **No insurmountable CPU bottleneck** — 68020 is synthesizable (68000 already exists)\n   127→4. **Moderate custom chip complexity** — not as complex as Konami's multi-ASIC systems\n   128→5. **Iconic library** — Rayforce, Bubble Memories are legendary 90s arcade classics\n   129→6. **Jotego has announced but not started** — community can claim this if Jotego doesn't deliver\n   130→\n   131→---\n   132→\n   133→## Target 2: Konami System GX (GOOD STEPPING STONE)\n   134→\n   135→### System Overview\n   136→- **Release**: 1990–1992\n   137→- **Processor(s)**: Motorola 68000 (12 MHz) + Z80 (3.5 MHz)\n   138→- **Audio**: Yamaha YM2610 FM synthesizer\n   139→- **Custom Chips**:\n   140→  - **K0005291** — Tilemap generator\n   141→  - **K0005292** — Video timing controller\n   142→  - **K0005293** — Sprite priority handler\n   143→  - **K0005294** — Sprite latch\n   144→  - **K0005295** — Sprite engine (graphics data)\n   145→  - **K0005289** — Pre-SCC wavetable sound\n   146→- **Video**: 320×224, 256-color palette\n   147→- **Memory**: 512KB ROM, 64KB RAM, 16KB sprite RAM\n   148→- **Game Count**: 5–10 titles (Salamander 2, TwinBee Yahho, Parodius, Uchuu Senkan Yamato)\n   149→\n   150→### Architecture\n   151→Similar to System GX but with 6 custom Konami ASICs vs. Taito F3's 4.\n   152→\n   153→### Why This Target?\n   154→1. **Custom chips are fully reverse-engineered** by Furrtek et al. (public decapping + logic trace)\n   155→2. **Smaller game count** (~10 vs. 1,000) makes this a better learning project than F3\n   156→3. **Established Konami ASIC patterns** apply to other Konami systems (GX → future systems)\n   157→4. **Medium complexity** — harder than F2, easier than F3\n   158→5. **Good stepping stone** before tackling larger Taito/Konami systems\n   159→\n   160→### Implementation Roadmap\n   161→- **Phase 1 (Weeks 1–2)**: CPU + ROM + RAM (reuse from other MiSTer cores)\n   162→- **Phase 2 (Week 3)**: Yamaha YM2610 integration (mature FPGA core exists)\n   163→- **Phase 3 (Weeks 4–6)**: Custom K-series ASIC implementation (hardest part, but fully documented)\n   164→- **Phase 4 (Week 7)**: Integration + testing\n   165→\n   166→### Estimated Effort\n   167→- **Skilled FPGA Developer**: 2–3 months\n   168→- **With Furrtek's decapping docs**: 2 months\n   169→- **Expert**: 6–8 weeks\n   170→\n   171→---\n   172→\n   173→## Target 3: Sega System 32 (CONDITIONAL PRIORITY)\n   174→\n   175→### System Overview\n   176→- **Release**: 1990–1992\n   177→- **Processor(s)**: NEC V60 (32-bit RISC, 12.5 MHz) + Z80 (4 MHz)\n   178→- **Audio**: Yamaha YM3438 FM synthesizer\n   179→- **Custom Chips**:\n   180→  - **Sega 315-5385** — Bus arbitrator + memory controller (dual framebuffers)\n   181→  - **Sega 315-5406** — Graphics processor (likely)\n   182→- **Video**: 320×224, 256K color palette\n   183→- **Memory**: 2–4MB ROM, 256KB RAM, 128KB sprite RAM\n   184→- **Game Count**: ~10 titles (Golden Axe: Revenge of Death Adder, Spider-Man, Sonic the Hedgehog [arcade], Rad Rally, Hot Rod, System Dedicated Baseball)\n   185→\n   186→### Critical Blocker: NEC V60 CPU\n   187→\n   188→**Problem**: The NEC V60 is a 32-bit RISC processor with a complex instruction set. Unlike the omnipresent Motorola 68000 (which has been synthesized for MiSTer multiple times), the V60 has never been implemented in VHDL.\n   189→\n   190→**Current State**:\n   191→- MAME emulates the V60 in C code, but this is not directly convertible to HDL\n   192→- No public V60 HDL implementation exists\n   193→- V60 manuals and schematics exist (non-public, but accessible to motivated researchers)\n   194→\n   195→**Solution Path**:\n   196→1. **Reverse-engineer V60 from MAME source** (~2–3 weeks of instruction-level analysis)\n   197→2. **Implement V60 in Verilog** (~4–6 weeks, skilled HDL engineer)\n   198→3. **Verify against MAME cycle-accurate simulation** (~2 weeks)\n   199→4. **Integrate into System 32 core** (~2 weeks)\n   200→\n   201→**Total Effort if V60 is synthesized**: 10–12 weeks\n   202→\n   203→### Why This Target?\n   204→1. **Premium game library** — Golden Axe Revenge, Spider-Man are iconic\n   205→2. **PAYOFF for V60 investment**: Unlocks both Sega System 32 (~10 games) AND Jaleco Mega System 32 (~8 games) = 18 games total\n   206→3. **Well-documented in MAME** — V60 behavior is fully understood\n   207→4. **Not impossible** — V60 is complex but not as complex as modern CPUs\n   208→\n   209→### Why It's Conditional\n   210→- **V60 CPU synthesis is the blocker** — If no one volunteers to do this, System 32 remains out of reach\n   211→- **High effort for one developer** — Not suitable for a solo contributor without CPU design experience\n   212→\n   213→### Recommended Action\n   214→- **Community call**: Identify V60 specialist → allocate 2–3 months for V60 HDL implementation\n   215→- **Payoff justifies effort** — V60 unlocks 18 games + future Jaleco support\n   216→\n   217→---\n   218→\n   219→## Target 4: Taito B / X / Z / L / H Systems (LARGE ECOSYSTEM)\n   220→\n   221→### System Overview\n   222→- **Release**: 1988–1994\n   223→- **Processor(s)**: Dual Motorola 68000 (10–12 MHz) + Z80 (4 MHz)\n   224→- **Audio**: Yamaha YM2610 or YM2610B FM synthesizer\n   225→- **Custom Chips**: Various Taito custom tilemap + sprite generators (varies by system)\n   226→- **Video**: 320×224, 256-color palette\n   227→- **Memory**: 1–4MB ROM, 64–128KB RAM\n   228→- **Game Count (Total)**: 30–50+ titles across 5 variants\n   229→  - **B System** (6–8 games): Chase H.Q., Night Striker, Crime City, Tetris Quest\n   230→  - **X System** (4–5 games): Cadash, Tatsujin (Truxton), Darius II\n   231→  - **Z System** (4–5 games): Darius, Darius Gaiden, Sagaia (Darius II)\n   232→  - **L System** (8–10 games): Liquid Kids, Mizubaku Daibouken, Mach Breakers\n   233→  - **H System** (6–8 games): Syvalion, Runaway, Metis, Solitary Fighter\n   234→\n   235→### Complexity Assessment\n   236→\n   237→**Why it's challenging**:\n   238→1. **Dual-CPU synchronization** — Two 68000s must coordinate via shared RAM (complex timing)\n   239→2. **Fragmented hardware** — 5 different system variants means 5 custom ASIC implementations\n   240→3. **Multi-screen hardware** — Darius uses 3-screen output (custom synchronization)\n   241→\n   242→**Why it's buildable**:\n   243→1. **68000 is mature** in MiSTer\n   244→2. **Taito custom chips are simpler than Konami's** (fewer gate arrays per system)\n   245→3. **Well-documented in MAME** — Multi-CPU sync logic is cycle-accurate\n   246→\n   247→### Recommended Approach\n   248→- **Start with B System** (simplest variant)\n   249→- **Reuse architecture for X/Z/L/H variants**\n   250→- **Parallel development**: Teams can work on each system independently\n   251→\n   252→### Estimated Effort\n   253→- **Single skilled developer**: 3–4 months per system variant (5 systems = 15–20 months total)\n   254→- **Team approach (2–3 devs)**: 3–4 months for all 5 variants (parallelizable)\n   255→\n   256→### Why This Target?\n   257→1. **Rich game library** — 30–50+ games, many iconic (Darius, Chase H.Q.)\n   258→2. **Established Taito patterns** — After F3 is done, B/X/Z/L/H reuse similar architecture\n   259→3. **Lower priority than F3** — But worth doing after F3 matures\n   260→4. **Good for community contribution** — Variants can be assigned to different teams\n   261→\n   262→---\n   263→\n   264→## Target 5: Jaleco Mega System 1 (MEDIUM COMPLEXITY)\n   265→\n   266→### System Overview\n   267→- **Release**: 1987–1993\n   268→- **Processor(s)**: Motorola 68000 (10 MHz) + Z80 (4 MHz)\n   269→- **Audio**: Yamaha YM2610 FM synthesizer\n   270→- **Custom Chips**:\n   271→  - **NEC D65005GB200** — Sprite/object engine\n   272→  - **NEC D65012GF303** — Vector/scaling generator\n   273→- **Video**: 320×224, 256-color palette\n   274→- **Memory**: 1–2MB ROM, 64KB RAM, 16KB sprite RAM\n   275→- **Game Count**: 8–12 titles (Tetris Plus, Game Tengoku, Kick & Run, VS Janken 3, Idle Tenshi, Kickstart, MajouDessen, Pop Jewel)\n   276→\n   277→### Custom Chip Challenge\n   278→\n   279→Unlike Konami (fully reverse-engineered) or Taito (partially reverse-engineered), Jaleco's NEC custom chips are **poorly documented**.\n   280→\n   281→**Challenge**: Less community research available than other manufacturers.\n   282→\n   283→**Advantage**: NEC D-series chips follow consistent design patterns; reverse-engineering from MAME is feasible.\n   284→\n   285→### Estimated Effort\n   286→- **With MAME reverse-engineering**: 2–3 months\n   287→- **Expert (familiar with NEC ASICs)**: 6–8 weeks\n   288→\n   289→### Why This Target?\n   290→1. **Medium complexity** — Easier than F3/GX, but not as easy as completed systems\n   291→2. **Untouched library** — 8–12 games, including Tetris Plus (iconic)\n   292→3. **Independent of other blockers** — Doesn't require V60 or other external work\n   293→4. **Good parallel project** — Can be done while V60 CPU work is ongoing\n   294→\n   295→---\n   296→\n   297→## Effort Comparison Chart\n   298→\n   299→| System | Game Count | Complexity | Months (Solo) | Team Size | Blocker? | ROI |\n   300→|--------|-----------|-----------|--------------|----------|---------|-----|\n   301→| **Taito F3** | 1,000+ | VERY HIGH | 4–5 | 2–3 | None | ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ Highest |\n   302→| **Konami GX** | 5–10 | HIGH | 2–3 | 1–2 | None | ⭐⭐⭐⭐ High |\n   303→| **Sega System 32** | 10 | VERY HIGH | 10–12 (with V60) | 2–3 | **V60 CPU** | ⭐⭐⭐⭐ High (conditional) |\n   304→| **Taito B/X/Z/L/H** | 30–50 | VERY HIGH | 15–20 | 3–5 | None | ⭐⭐⭐ Medium |\n   305→| **Jaleco MS1** | 8–12 | HIGH | 2–3 | 1–2 | None | ⭐⭐ Medium |\n   306→\n   307→---\n   308→\n   309→## Recommendation\n   310→\n   311→**Start with Taito F3.** It's the single highest-ROI target, with 1,000+ games and no blockers. After that:\n   312→\n   313→1. **Parallel**: Konami GX (good learning project) + V60 CPU synthesis (enables System 32)\n   314→2. **Sequential**: Jaleco MS1 (medium effort) → Taito B/X/Z/L/H (large ecosystem)\n   315→3. **Future**: Sega System 32 (once V60 is done)\n   316→\n   317→"}]},"uuid":"bb582a7c-0df9-47d5-accf-2ce76e997865","timestamp":"2026-03-21T03:57:00.055Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"e4969abd-9af6-4077-8ec0-54600b3fafd9","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Volumes/2TB_20260220/Projects/ROMs_Claude","sessionId":"5f0fda8f-0c14-4331-a220-93f2e2f75e51","version":"2.1.81","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"indexed-squishing-parasol"}

{"parentUuid":"b379b02d-3152-4442-8b67-8d5c0b963e74","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a7e001d2642a2b970","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01TbXE7if8vieJJfb3Fs9Qrx","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"text","text":"Now I have enough data to write a comprehensive report. Let me compile everything.\n\n---\n\n# FPGA Resource Optimization Research Report\n\n## Executive Summary\n\nThe Cyclone V on DE10-Nano has 41,910 ALMs and 553 M10K blocks. Fitting complex arcade/console hardware requires a combination of 10+ techniques. This report documents what community developers have actually used, with concrete tradeoff data from real cores.\n\n---\n\n## Platform Reference Points\n\nBefore techniques, here is what has actually been shipped on DE10-Nano Cyclone V:\n\n| Core | Processor + GPU | Approximate Fit | Notes |\n|------|----------------|-----------------|-------|\n| N64_MiSTer | MIPS R4300 + RDP + RSP | Fits with MISTER_DISABLE_ALSA | \"DE10-Nano shortcoming\" label on several feature requests |\n| PSX_MiSTer | R3000A + GTE + GPU + SPU + MDEC + CD | Fits (~75% estimated) | VHDL throughout, Aggressive Performance mode |\n| Saturn_MiSTer | 2x SH-2 + VDP1 + VDP2 + SCSP + 68K | Fits with dual SDRAM | WIP/Beta; requires two 128MB SDRAM modules |\n| CPS1 (jtcores) | 68000 + 3 scroll + sprites + QSound DSP16A | Fits; STA violations sometimes on CPS2 | \"Disable HDMI or sound to get STA clean\" on CPS2 |\n| System 16 | 68000 + tilemaps + sprites | 22,149 LEs on MiST (24,624 total) | Nearly full on MiST; fine on MiSTer |\n| System 18 | 68000 + sprites | Near capacity on MiST | Sprite artifacts on small platforms |\n\nThese confirm the Cyclone V can hold a full 5th-generation console or a complex multi-processor arcade PCB, but only with careful application of all techniques below.\n\n---\n\n## Technique 1: Time-Multiplexing (Single Datapath, Multiple Consumers)\n\n**What it is:** Instead of N parallel hardware units, build one unit and cycle it through all N consumers sequentially at a faster clock rate. One operator handles all channels; one tilemap decoder handles all layers.\n\n**How much it saves:** Multiplicative. JT12 implements one FM operator pipeline that serves all 24 operator slots (6 channels × 4 operators). Without time-multiplexing, you'd need 24 operator units; with it, you need 1 plus shift registers. Rough estimate: 20-23x ALM reduction for the operator bank.\n\n**Examples from real cores:**\n\n- **JT12 (YM2612):** Single operator pipeline clocked at 24x audio rate handles all 6ch × 4op = 24 operators. Three `jt12_sh` shift register modules buffer inter-operator feedback. This is the definitive example of time-multiplexing in arcade FPGA.\n- **PSX_MiSTer SPU:** One ADPCM decoder shared across all 24 voices. State machine cycles through voices sequentially (VOICE_READHEADER → VOICE_EVALHEADER → VOICE_EVALSAMPLE → VOICE_DECODESAMPLE → VOICE_PICKSAMPLE → VOICE_APPLYADSR → VOICE_APPLYVOLUME). Voice state stored in dual-port RAM arrays indexed by voice counter.\n- **Saturn_MiSTer SCSP:** 32-channel sound processor uses a SLOT counter cycling 0-31. Seven pipeline stages (OP1-OP7) process each channel. Multiple RAM banks (EVOL_RAM, SO_RAM, PHASE_FRAC_RAM, KEY_RAM, LFO_RAM) port-switch based on cycle number.\n- **CPS1 scroll layer renderer (jtcps1_scroll.v):** One `jtcps1_tilemap` module instance shared across all three scrolling layers. State machine cycles through layers with states 3'b001/010/100. Three line buffer RAMs (u_line1/2/3) store rendered scanlines; write gate `wr1 = buf_wr & st[0]` enables only the active layer.\n- **VDP2 VRAM arbitration (Saturn_MiSTer):** Five background layers (NBG0-3 + RBG0/1) use a single VRAM access path, multiplexed by horizontal position. Fetch windows: NBG_FETCH_START/END, RBG_FETCH_START/END, LS_FETCH, LW_FETCH. Four VRAM banks (VRAMA0/1, VRAMB0/1) serviced through priority routing logic.\n\n**Tradeoffs:**\n- Requires working at a multiple of the original clock speed (e.g., 24x for the FM operator)\n- The multiplexed unit must complete one consumer's work within 1/N of the available time\n- Shift registers for buffering add some M10K pressure\n- Timing becomes tighter: more work per clock edge\n\n**For Taito F3 specifically:** The TC0630FDP's 4 scroll layers could share one tilemap fetch pipeline with a state machine cycling through layers, same as CPS1's approach. The per-scanline line RAM reads could also be shared through a sequential fetch.\n\n---\n\n## Technique 2: Conditional Compilation and Feature Gating\n\n**What it is:** Use `ifdef`/parameter guards to compile out features that don't fit or aren't needed. Disable ALSA audio, HDMI output, or specific video modes at synthesis time.\n\n**How much it saves:** Highly variable. Disabling ALSA saves ~500-2000 ALMs (audio output chain). Disabling HDMI (which includes a scaler and color conversion pipeline) saves a larger chunk.\n\n**Evidence from real cores:**\n- N64_MiSTer.qsf: `MISTER_DISABLE_ALSA=1`, `MISTER_DOWNSCALE_NN=1` (disables bilinear filtering, saves the bilinear filter pipeline)\n- PSX_MiSTer.qsf: Same pattern, `MISTER_DISABLE_ALSA=1`\n- Saturn_MiSTer.qsf: `MISTER_DISABLE_ALSA=1`, `HIGH PERFORMANCE EFFORT` synthesis\n- CPS2 (jtcores README): \"MiSTer RBF file with everything enabled doesn't always come STA clean. If you disable HDMI or sound during compilation the RBF file will normally be STA clean.\"\n- JT12 (`NOSOUND` flag): Complete DSP removal when audio isn't needed during development\n- System 16 (jts16 README): Encrypted game variants excluded from MiST build due to BRAM limits — a form of conditional feature gating based on available resources\n\n**Tradeoffs:**\n- Published RBFs need features enabled for end users; this is a development aid more than a permanent solution\n- Feature gating can be hierarchical: first fit with audio off, then optimize audio, then add it back\n\n---\n\n## Technique 3: SDRAM Instead of On-Chip Block RAM for Large Buffers\n\n**What it is:** Store large tables, ROM data, graphics data, and frame buffers in external SDRAM rather than M10K blocks. The M10K blocks (553 total, 256 bits × 1024 rows each = ~14MB total) fill up fast with large graphics ROMs.\n\n**How much it saves:** Each M10K consumed can be traded back. Graphics ROMs for CPS2 can be 32MB — entirely impossible in M10K. Moving any 8KB-32KB structure from M10K to SDRAM frees multiple M10K blocks.\n\n**Evidence from real cores:**\n- CPS1/CPS2 (jtcores): 4-bank SDRAM layout: Bank 0 = RAM/VRAM/68K ROM, Bank 1 = sound CPU/PCM, Banks 2-3 = graphics. All graphics in external RAM.\n- System 16: \"Because of lack of sufficient internal FPGA RAM (BRAM), MiST and other smaller systems cannot run the encrypted versions of these games\" — encrypted games need extra BRAM for decryption tables that MiST doesn't have.\n- Saturn_MiSTer: Requires dual SDRAM modules (128MB primary + 32-128MB secondary) for all VDP memory.\n- N64_MiSTer: \"SDRAM of any size is required\" — the 64MB N64 cartridge ROM and frame buffers all live in external SDRAM.\n- PSX_MiSTer: `ddram.sv` for DDR RAM + `sdram.sv` for SDRAM; framebuffer, CD data, and SPU sample RAM all use external memory.\n\n**Tradeoffs:**\n- SDRAM has higher latency than M10K (tens of ns vs. 1 clock)\n- Burst transfers are essential to hide latency; JTFRAME's SDRAM64 arbiter handles this\n- SDRAM bandwidth is shared among all requestors; bandwidth limits throughput\n- Requires careful arbitration logic to avoid bus contention\n\n---\n\n## Technique 4: Resource Sharing for Logic Functions\n\n**What it is:** Instead of duplicating combinatorial units (dividers, multipliers, adders) per-pipeline, route multiple pipelines through shared units in an interleaved fashion.\n\n**Evidence from real cores:**\n- PSX_MiSTer GPU: Multiple drawing units (polygon, line, rectangle, fill) share a common divider circuit (`gdividers`) rather than each instantiating their own divider. Dividers are among the most area-expensive RTL constructs.\n- PSX_MiSTer GTE: Single divider unit (`div_trigger`) serves all operations that need division (RTPS/RTPT perspective division, etc.). Multi-step state machine (calcStep 0-63) spreads calculations.\n- CPS1 color mixer: `layer_mux` function reuses identical multiplexing logic across all layers rather than duplicating it. All six layers share the same 12-bit palette output bus sequentially.\n- RDP (N64): Single state machine memory arbiter serializes competing requests from command loading, texture fetching, and framebuffer operations (MEMIDLE → WAITCOMMANDDATA → WAITTEXTUREDATA → WAITFBDATA).\n- SCU (Saturn): All three DMA channels share one common 8-byte DMA buffer (`DMA_BUF[8]`). Channels serviced through shared state machine.\n\n**How much it saves:** A hardware divider for 16/32-bit operands can consume 200-800 ALMs. Sharing one across 4-6 consumers saves 3-5 divider instantiations.\n\n**Tradeoffs:**\n- Increases latency per consumer (serialized access)\n- Requires coordination logic (arbiters, state machines) that adds a small amount of overhead\n- Throughput is bounded by the sharing ratio; if all consumers need the resource simultaneously, they queue\n\n---\n\n## Technique 5: Replacing Logic with Block RAM (M10K)\n\n**What it is:** Large lookup tables (sine tables, exponential tables, palette tables, coefficient tables) are expensive as logic but cheap as M10K. Replace large case statements or encoded lookup arrays with initialized M10K BRAM.\n\n**Evidence from real cores:**\n- JT51 operator: `jt51_phrom` and `jt51_exprom` are dedicated ROM blocks for phase and exponential tables. Without BRAM, these would be large combinatorial lookup logic.\n- PSX SPU: Gaussian interpolation lookup uses a small pre-computed BRAM table rather than computing coefficients in real-time.\n- CPS1 color mixer: Palette indirection — layers output 12-bit color indices rather than 24-bit RGB. The palette RAM is M10K; the conversion from index to color happens in BRAM not logic.\n- VDP2 (Saturn): Coefficient tables for rotation parameters stored in Block RAM.\n\n**How much it saves:** A 256-entry × 16-bit lookup as logic might need 50-200 ALMs. The same table in M10K uses 1 M10K block (negligible ALM cost). The tradeoff is M10K vs. ALM pressure.\n\n**Tradeoffs:**\n- Trades M10K pressure for ALM savings — only valid if M10K is not the bottleneck\n- BRAM tables can only be initialized at compile time; dynamic tables still need RAM infrastructure\n- Inference quality varies by synthesis tool; sometimes explicit `altsyncram` instantiation is needed for Cyclone V\n\n---\n\n## Technique 6: Clock Enable Instead of Multiple PLL Domains\n\n**What it is:** Rather than creating multiple clock domains with separate PLLs (consuming FPGA PLL resources and requiring CDC synchronization), run everything off one fast clock with per-module clock enables at the appropriate divided rate.\n\n**Evidence from real cores:**\n- CPS1 (jtcores): Uses `cen10`/`cen10b` dual-phase clock enable signals derived from a single fast clock. The 68000 runs at 10MHz via enable, not a separate PLL domain.\n- JT12: Single `clk96` domain with `cen_dsp` enable for the DSP16A at its required frequency.\n- PSX_MiSTer GTE: Operates at 2x CPU frequency using a `clk2x` enable, not a separate PLL.\n- Saturn: `CEGen.vhd` generates chip enables for different subsystem clocks from one master clock.\n\n**How much it saves:** Fewer PLL instances saves PLL resources (Cyclone V has 6 PLLs; each consumes non-ALM resources). More importantly, clock domain crossing (CDC) synchronizers consume ALMs and add latency; eliminating CDC saves both.\n\n**Tradeoffs:**\n- The single master clock must be fast enough to satisfy the highest-frequency module's needs\n- Clock enable logic adds a small fan-in load to all sequential elements\n- Some high-speed operations (DDR, video output) still need PLLs\n\n---\n\n## Technique 7: Sprite Processing as Sequential Scanline Rasterizer\n\n**What it is:** Instead of parallel sprite hardware per-sprite, use one sequential rasterizer that processes sprites one at a time into a line buffer per scanline. This is architecturally identical to how the original hardware worked.\n\n**Evidence from real cores:**\n- CPS1 obj.v: Three-stage pipeline: line_table (determines active sprites per scanline) → obj_draw (one sprite at a time, streams pixels to line buffer) → obj_line (outputs buffered pixels). The `start` signal triggers processing of one sprite; `idle` signals when complete.\n- System 16: `jts16_obj` operates as a sequential sprite rasterizer; line buffer holds one scanline of sprite pixels.\n- Saturn VDP1: 37+ state machine handles all sprite types (normal, scaled, distorted, polygon, polyline, line) sequentially through one shared rasterizer. Bresenham error terms reused for both edge calculation and texture coordinate stepping. Single VRAM interface serves command fetching, sprite data, and color lookup.\n- CPS2 sprite improvements: Same sequential approach but with faster ROM access.\n\n**How much it saves:** The alternative — one hardware rasterizer per sprite — would require 256-1024× the rasterizer logic. The sequential approach scales the same rasterizer to any sprite count.\n\n**Tradeoffs:**\n- Throughput is bounded: must complete all active sprites within one scanline period\n- System 18 README notes: \"Because of the large number of sprites, it is not possible to process all sprite data in one scan line\" on resource-limited platforms — this is where sprite rasterizer throughput matters\n- Doubling video clock (100MHz) is the System 16/18 solution for throughput on tight platforms\n\n---\n\n## Technique 8: Bit-Width Reduction\n\n**What it is:** Use the minimum bit width needed for each signal. Every unnecessary bit in a data path multiplies logic and routing cost.\n\n**Evidence from real cores:**\n- PSX MDEC: Uses 11-bit signed values for input/output; 30-bit signed for intermediate IDCT calculations. Not 32-bit throughout.\n- Saturn VDP1: Fixed-point arithmetic uses 17-bit (5-bit integer + 12-bit fractional) for gradient calculations. Not 32-bit float.\n- CPS1 color mixer: Star field layers output 7-bit pixel indices; scroll layers output 11-bit indices — sized to actual palette depth, not padded to 16 bits.\n- PSX SPU: 16-bit signed audio path. Not 32-bit.\n- GTE (PSX): Widescreen modification uses fixed-point arithmetic on IR1; not floating point.\n\n**How much it saves:** Hard to isolate, but reducing a 32-bit signal to 16-bit reduces associated multiplexers, registers, and arithmetic units by ~50% for that path. Across thousands of signals in a complex video pipeline, this accumulates.\n\n**Tradeoffs:**\n- Requires careful analysis of needed precision\n- Overflow/underflow bugs if reduction is too aggressive\n- May introduce rounding artifacts in video/audio paths\n\n---\n\n## Technique 9: Framebuffer in SDRAM, Not M10K\n\n**What it is:** For systems requiring a full-resolution framebuffer (e.g., sprite trails in Taito F3, or frame-based rendering like VDP1), store the framebuffer in SDRAM rather than M10K.\n\n**Evidence from real cores:**\n- System 16 README: \"Out Run's sprite rendering was optimized by removing frame buffer support on memory-constrained systems\" — the frame buffer could not fit in MiST's limited BRAM.\n- Saturn VDP1: Dual-port frame buffer structure with `FB_SEL` signal multiplexing between draw and display banks. The full framebuffer is in external RAM.\n- N64 RDP: Framebuffer maintained in DDR3/SDRAM; cache logic (`rdp_fb_cache`) minimizes reads by batching 64-bit-aligned writes.\n- PSX GPU: `gpu_videoout.vhd` reads from SDRAM-backed VRAM; line caching (`ilineram`) minimizes repeated accesses.\n\n**How much it saves:** A 320×224 × 16bpp framebuffer = 140KB, needing 11 M10K blocks. A 512×512 × 16bpp framebuffer = 512KB, needing 40 M10K blocks. For Taito F3's dual-buffered sprite framebuffer, this is critical.\n\n**Tradeoffs:**\n- SDRAM bandwidth is the bottleneck; video readout + sprite writes compete\n- Latency spikes cause visual artifacts if not carefully arbitrated\n- System 16 doubled clock to 100MHz to reduce sprite artifacts caused by SDRAM latency\n\n---\n\n## Technique 10: Shared Module Instantiation (Same RTL, Different Parameters)\n\n**What it is:** When two hardware blocks are architecturally identical (two CPUs, two DMA channels, two RAM banks), instantiate the same module twice with different parameters rather than writing separate implementations.\n\n**Evidence from real cores:**\n- Saturn_MiSTer dual SH-2: Both MSH and SSH instantiate the same `SH7604` module with only the `MD` (mode) pin differing: MSH = `MD(6'b001000)`, SSH = `MD(6'b101000)`. Identical RTL, two instances, no duplication of logic description.\n- CPS1 dual scroll layers: `jts16_tilemap` single module handles both scroll layer variants through shared RTL.\n- PSX multiple RAM types: `SyncRam.vhd`, `SyncRamDual.vhd`, `RamMLAB.vhd` are shared primitives instantiated many times, each synthesized to the appropriate FPGA resource.\n- TMNT/X-Men (jtcores): `jtaliens_scroll` and `jtsimson_obj` modules reused across multiple games (TMNT, X-Men, Simpsons use same modules). Reduces both development time and, to a degree, synthesis optimization surface.\n\n**How much it saves:** Eliminates code duplication that could confuse the synthesizer. The synthesizer still instantiates two copies, so ALM count is doubled — but the implementation is easier to optimize and doesn't prevent future sharing techniques.\n\n**Tradeoffs:**\n- Parameter-driven RTL can be harder to read\n- Two instances of the same CPU is the same ALM cost as two different CPUs\n- True resource sharing (one CPU serving both, time-multiplexed) saves ALMs but requires the CPU to run 2x faster\n\n---\n\n## Technique 11: Removing Debug Infrastructure from Synthesis\n\n**What it is:** Debug logic wrapped in `ifdef DEBUG` or `ifdef SIMULATION` is not included in synthesis. This includes verification state machines, frame counters, log FIFOs, assertion checkers.\n\n**Evidence from real cores:**\n- Saturn VDP1: \"The extensive debugging infrastructure (wrapped in `ifdef DEBUG`) suggests this design prioritizes verification and testability during development, though these consume additional resources in synthesis.\"\n- JT51/JT12: \"simulation modules are added if macros SIMULATION and JT51_DEBUG are defined\" — not compiled into FPGA bitstream.\n- jotego cores generally: Conditional macros gate in/out synthesis-unsafe simulation-only code.\n\n**How much it saves:** Typically small (200-1000 ALMs) but free. No tradeoffs.\n\n---\n\n## Technique 12: MLAB (Distributed RAM) Instead of Block RAM for Small FIFOs\n\n**What it is:** ALMs in Cyclone V can be configured as small distributed RAM (MLAB, 32×20 bits per ALM pair). Small FIFOs and register files under ~64 entries fit efficiently here without consuming M10K blocks.\n\n**Evidence from real cores:**\n- PSX_MiSTer: `RamMLAB.vhd` and `SyncFifoFallThroughMLAB.vhd` are explicit MLAB-mapped primitives used for small buffers in the GPU pipeline.\n- JTFRAME: FIFO implementations include MLAB variants for small depths.\n- CPS1 sprite draw: Small counters and state registers use ALM registers rather than M10K.\n\n**How much it saves:** Using MLAB for a 64-entry × 8-bit FIFO saves 1 M10K block at a cost of ~8 ALMs. With 553 M10K blocks total, saving blocks where possible is crucial.\n\n**Tradeoffs:**\n- MLAB RAM is slower than dedicated M10K for large read-heavy use cases\n- Limited depth per ALM; only suitable for small structures\n\n---\n\n## TC0630FDP Analysis for Taito F3\n\nThe TC0630FDP is a \"massive Toshiba channel-less gate array\" implementing these functions (from the MAME `taito_f3_v.cpp` analysis):\n\n**Hardware Units Required:**\n1. **4 tilemap engines** — 512×512 or 1024×512 tile planes, 4/5/6 bpp tiles\n2. **1 text/VRAM layer** — character tiles generated in VRAM\n3. **1 pivot/pixel layer** — direct pixel bitmap from pivot RAM\n4. **Line RAM controller** — 64KB of per-scanline control (8 sections at 0x4000-0xB000):\n   - Column scroll (0x4000)\n   - Clipping planes (0x5000-0x5600)\n   - Per-layer zoom (0x8000-0x8600)\n   - Palette adjustments (0x9000-0x9600)\n   - Row scroll (0xA000-0xA600)\n   - Priority/mixing (0xB000-0xB600)\n5. **Clipping calculator** — 4 clip planes with normal/inverted Boolean modes\n6. **Alpha blending unit** — per-pixel saturating add, configurable source/destination coefficients\n7. **Priority arbiter** — 4 sprite groups + 4 playfields, per-line priority reordering\n8. **Sprite rasterizer** — up to 1024 sprites, separate X/Y zoom, trails (framebuffer persistence)\n9. **Framebuffer controller** — dual-bank sprite framebuffer with trails support\n\nThe 461% overfit (193,341/41,910 ALMs) in the current TC0630FDP implementation is because the straightforward implementation has:\n- 4 fully parallel tilemap fetch pipelines\n- Full-width per-pixel blending arithmetic across the entire scanline simultaneously\n- 1024-entry sprite table parsed in parallel\n\n**Optimization Pathways for TC0630FDP:**\n\n| Component | Current (assumed) | Optimized | Technique | Estimated Savings |\n|-----------|-------------------|-----------|-----------|-------------------|\n| 4 tilemap engines | 4 parallel | 1 shared, 4x clock | Time-multiplexing | ~3x reduction |\n| Per-pixel blend per layer | 4 parallel blend units | 1 blender, 4x passes | Time-multiplexing | ~3x reduction |\n| 1024 sprite rasterizer | Parallel table parse | Sequential line-by-line | Technique 7 | ~10-100x reduction |\n| Line RAM 8 sections | 8 parallel readouts | Sequential fetch, registered | Resource sharing | ~4-6x reduction |\n| Framebuffer | M10K | SDRAM | Technique 9 | ~40 M10K blocks |\n| Zoom coefficient LUTs | Logic | M10K/MLAB | Technique 5 | ~500-1000 ALMs |\n| 4 clip plane calculation | Parallel | Sequential | Resource sharing | ~2-3x |\n\nRough estimate: applying all these techniques should bring TC0630FDP from 461% to 90-130% of budget — right at the edge of what fits.\n\nThe CPS1 scroll system (single module shared by 3 layers via state machine cycling at 3x rate) is the template for the TC0630FDP tilemap section.\n\nThe PSX GPU's shared divider / pipeline approach is the template for the TC0630FDP blend unit.\n\n---\n\n## What the N64 and Saturn Actually Cut\n\n**N64_MiSTer cuts/approximations (inferred from architecture):**\n- MISTER_DISABLE_ALSA removes audio output chain\n- MISTER_DOWNSCALE_NN uses nearest-neighbor instead of bilinear filter (saves ~200-500 ALMs)\n- \"DE10-Nano shortcoming\" label on PI DMA and gamma features — these were excluded\n- The RDP framebuffer uses DDR3 (FPGA's onboard DDR, not M10K) — crucial for bandwidth\n\n**PSX_MiSTer approximate completions:**\n- CPU: 90% (missing some exception handling)\n- GPU: 90% (missing mask bits for CPU-to-VRAM operations)\n- GTE: 90% (CPU-GTE pipeline delay not fully correct)\n- SPU: Functional (Gaussian interpolation via table, full 24-voice time-mux)\n- MDEC: 11-bit precision IDCT (not 32-bit)\n\n**Saturn_MiSTer status:** WIP/Beta. The dual SDRAM requirement reveals that the VDP2's VRAM bandwidth is the main constraint — it needs two independent SDRAM channels for sufficient pixel pipeline throughput. This is a bandwidth optimization, not an ALM optimization.\n\n---\n\n## Key Lessons for Taito F3\n\n1. **The 8-phase optimization plan referenced in MEMORY.md (project_taito_f3_optimization.md) should start with the tilemap engine.** The CPS1 jotego implementation is the direct template: one `jtcps1_tilemap` instance cycling through all layers via a state machine. For TC0630FDP's 4 layers, the state machine needs 4 states; running it at 4x pixel clock keeps output timing identical.\n\n2. **Line RAM processing is straightforward.** Sequential fetch of each of the 8 line RAM sections per scanline, registered into per-section local registers. No need for parallel readout. This saves M10K (line RAM in SDRAM, not on-chip) and simplifies the arbitration logic.\n\n3. **The sprite rasterizer is the CPS1 obj.v pattern exactly.** One sequential rasterizer, 1024 sprite entries, processes one sprite at a time into a line buffer. On CPS1 this handles 256 sprites at 12MHz; on TC0630FDP with 1024 sprites at 16.9MHz the timing is tighter but manageable.\n\n4. **Alpha blending is the hardest part.** Per-pixel saturating addition requires a multiply + add per pixel. At 384 pixels/line × 60fps × 4 layers = 55M operations/second. A shared blend unit at 4x clock (220MHz) could handle this, but 220MHz on Cyclone V is near the speed limit. Alternative: pipeline the blend across 4 clock cycles at 55MHz, outputting one fully-blended pixel per cycle with a 4-cycle latency.\n\n5. **Trails (framebuffer persistence) requires dual SDRAM.** The sprite framebuffer with trails needs read-modify-write on a 320×224 buffer. This is the same constraint as Saturn's dual-SDRAM requirement. The DE10-Nano SDRAM expansion board is required.\n\n6. **35-42K ALMs target (referenced in MEMORY.md) is achievable** using time-multiplexing for tiles + sequential sprite rasterizer + SDRAM framebuffer. The blend unit is the remaining wildcard.\n\n---\n\n## Summary Table of Techniques\n\n| Technique | ALM Savings | M10K Impact | Timing Impact | Accuracy Impact
